        <title>Draft genome sequence of the rubber tree Hevea brasiliensis | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Hevea brasiliensis, a member of the Euphorbiaceae family, is the major commercial source of natural rubber (NR). NR is a latex polymer with high elasticity, flexibility, and resilience that has played a critical role in the world economy since 1876. Here, we report the draft genome sequence of H. brasiliensis. The assembly spans ~1.1 Gb of the estimated 2.15 Gb haploid genome. Overall, ~78% of the genome was identified as repetitive DNA. Gene prediction shows 68,955 gene models, of which 12.7% are unique to Hevea. Most of the key genes associated with rubber biosynthesis, rubberwood formation, disease resistance, and allergenicity have been identified. The knowledge gained from this genome sequence will aid in the future development of high-yielding clones to keep up with the ever increasing need for natural rubber."/>

    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

    })();

        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Draft genome sequence of the rubber tree Hevea brasiliensis"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Hevea brasiliensis, a member of the Euphorbiaceae family, is the major commercial source of natural rubber (NR). NR is a latex polymer with high elasticity, flexibility, and resilience that has played a critical role in the world economy since 1876. Here, we report the draft genome sequence of H. brasiliensis. The assembly spans ~1.1 Gb of the estimated 2.15 Gb haploid genome. Overall, ~78% of the genome was identified as repetitive DNA. Gene prediction shows 68,955 gene models, of which 12.7% are unique to Hevea. Most of the key genes associated with rubber biosynthesis, rubberwood formation, disease resistance, and allergenicity have been identified. The knowledge gained from this genome sequence will aid in the future development of high-yielding clones to keep up with the ever increasing need for natural rubber."/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p><i>Hevea brasiliensis,</i> a member of the Euphorbiaceae family, is the major commercial source of natural rubber (NR). NR is a latex polymer with high elasticity, flexibility, and resilience that has played a critical role in the world economy since 1876.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Here, we report the draft genome sequence of <i>H. brasiliensis</i>. The assembly spans ~1.1 Gb of the estimated 2.15 Gb haploid genome. Overall, ~78% of the genome was identified as repetitive DNA. Gene prediction shows 68,955 gene models, of which 12.7% are unique to <i>Hevea</i>. Most of the key genes associated with rubber biosynthesis, rubberwood formation, disease resistance, and allergenicity have been identified.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>The knowledge gained from this genome sequence will aid in the future development of high-yielding clones to keep up with the ever increasing need for natural rubber.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Rubber is an indispensable commodity used in the manufacture of over 50,000 products worldwide [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Prabhakaran Nair KP: The Agronomy and Economy of Important Tree Crops of the Developing World. 2010, Burlington: Elsevier" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1357">1</a>]. Approximately 2,500 plant species synthesize rubber [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mooibroek H, Cornish K: Alternative sources of natural rubber. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2000, 53: 355-365. 10.1007/s002530051627." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1360">2</a>], but <i>Hevea brasiliensis</i> (Willd.) Muell.-Arg., also known as Pará rubber tree, is the primary commercial source for natural rubber (NR) production. This member of the Euphorbiaceae family originated from the Amazon Basin, and it was not until the nineteenth century that it significantly began to be commercially exploited and its domestic cultivation was established outside of South America. Today, plantations are mainly found in the tropical regions of Asia, Africa, and Latin America. Rubber trees start yielding latex after reaching 5–7 years of maturity and have a productive lifespan of 25–30 years. According to the International Rubber Study Group (<a href="http://www.rubberstudy.com">http://www.rubberstudy.com</a>), global production of NR reached nearly 11 million tons in 2011 with Asia accounting for about 93% of the supply. The demand for rubber (natural and synthetic) has steadily risen over the past century and is expected to continue to increase.</p><p>NR is a latex polymer with high elasticity, flexibility, resilience, impact resistance, and efficient heat dispersion [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mooibroek H, Cornish K: Alternative sources of natural rubber. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2000, 53: 355-365. 10.1007/s002530051627." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1376">2</a>]. These properties make NR difficult to be replaced by synthetic rubber in many applications, such as medical gloves and heavy-duty tires for aircrafts and trucks. NR consists of 94% <i>cis</i>-1,4-polyisoprene and 6% proteins and fatty acids [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Sakdapipanich JT: Structural characterization of natural rubber based on recent evidence from selective enzymatic treatments. J Biosci Bioeng. 2007, 103: 287-292. 10.1263/jbb.103.287." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1382">3</a>]. <i>Cis</i>-1,4-polyisoprene biopolymers are made up of C5 monomeric isopentenyl diphosphate (IPP) units and are formed by sequential condensation on the surface of rubber particles. The rubber chain elongation is catalyzed by <i>cis</i>-prenyltransferases (CPTs), known as rubber polymerases [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Asawatreratanakul K, Zhang YW, Wititsuwannakul D, Wititsuwannakul R, Takahashi S, Rattanapittayaporn A, Koyama T: Molecular cloning, expression and characterization of cDNA encoding cis-prenyltransferases from Hevea brasiliensis: a key factor participating in natural rubber biosynthesis. Eur J Biochem. 2003, 270: 4671-4680. 10.1046/j.1432-1033.2003.03863.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1392">4</a>]. The molecular weight of the resulting polymer is an important determinant of rubber quality. Only a few plants produce large amounts of high quality NR (molecular weight &gt; 1 million daltons), including <i>H. brasiliensis</i> and the potential alternative rubber crops <i>Parthenium argentatum</i> (guayule) and <i>Taraxacum koksaghyz</i> (Russian dandelion) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Gronover CS, Wahler D, Prüfer D: Natural rubber biosynthesis and physic-chemical studies on plant derived latex. Biotechnology of Biopolymers. Edited by: Elnashar M. 2011, Croatia: Intech Open Acess Publisher, 75-88." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1404">5</a>].</p><p>In addition to NR, rubber trees are used as a source of timber, once their latex productivity is no longer economically viable. Rubberwood has become a major timber export of Southeast Asia [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Prabhakaran Nair KP: The Agronomy and Economy of Important Tree Crops of the Developing World. 2010, Burlington: Elsevier" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1410">1</a>]. Its natural light color and excellent physical properties make it suitable for flooring and household furniture. Owing to the value of this product, several superior latex-timber clones have been developed.</p><p>Some of the issues concerning the rubber industry include pathogen attack and allergenicity. Fungal diseases, such as South American Leaf Blight (SALB; caused by <i>Microcyclus ulei</i>) and leaf fall caused by <i>Colletotrichum, Oidium</i>, and <i>Corynespora</i>, are major threats to rubber production [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Prabhakaran Nair KP: The Agronomy and Economy of Important Tree Crops of the Developing World. 2010, Burlington: Elsevier" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1425">1</a>]. In the mid-1930s, SALB collapsed the rubber industry in Brazil. Asian plantations have not been hit by this disease yet, but an outbreak in the region could have devastating effects. The allergenicity of NR is an issue which continues to be a global medical concern for those repeatedly exposed to latex-containing products (e.g., gloves). These allergies are triggered by certain proteins present in <i>Hevea</i>-derived NR. In recent years, guayule has emerged as a source of hypoallergenic latex [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mooibroek H, Cornish K: Alternative sources of natural rubber. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2000, 53: 355-365. 10.1007/s002530051627." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1432">2</a>].</p><p>Difficulties with conventional breeding along with limited genome-based information have impeded efficient crop improvement of <i>H. brasiliensis</i>. Marker assisted selection can improve the efficiency of breeding by enabling the direct selection of targeted genotypes. Analysis of genetic linkage among markers and identification of the genetic locations of desirable phenotypes would further improve the selection accuracy. A recent surge in high-throughput sequencing efforts [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Triwitayakorn K, Chatkulkawin P, Kanjanawattanawong S, Sraphet S, Yoocha T, Sangsrakru D, Chanprasert J, Ngamphiw C, Jomchai N, Therawattanasuk K, Tangphatsornruang S: Transcriptome sequencing of Hevea brasiliensis for development of microsatellite markers and construction of a genetic linkage map. DNA Res. 2011, 18: 471-482. 10.1093/dnares/dsr034." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1442">6</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Pootakham W, Chanprasert J, Jomchai N, Sangsrakru D, Yoocha T, Tragoonrung S, Tangphatsornruang S: Development of genomic-derived simple sequence repeat markers in Hevea brasiliensis from 454 genome shotgun sequences. Plant Breeding. 2012, 131: 555-562. 10.1111/j.1439-0523.2012.01982.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1445">12</a>] has enhanced the genetic resources available for <i>H. brasiliensis</i>. However, whole-genome information is still lacking. While most of the studies have focused on transcriptome analysis, the non-coding regions of the genome are also essential for understanding the regulatory elements controlling gene expression, as well as for the development of a more comprehensive set of molecular markers. Here, we report the draft genome of <i>H. brasiliensis</i>, which provides a platform to help accelerate the future improvement of this economically important crop.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and annotation</h3><p>We sequenced the genome of <i>H. brasiliensis</i> clone RRIM 600, a high yielding clone developed by the Rubber Research Institute of Malaysia (parentage: Tjir 1 × PB 86). The rubber tree genome is distributed over 18 pairs of chromosomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Leitch AR, Lim KY, Leitch IJ, O’Neill M, Chye ML, Low FC: Molecular cytogenetic studies in rubber, Hevea brasiliensis Muell. Arg. (Euphorbiaceae). Genome. 1998, 41: 464-467." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1469">13</a>], with the haploid genome estimated to be ~2.15 Gb by Feulgen microdensitometry [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Bennett MD, Leitch IJ: Nuclear DNA amounts in angiosperms-583 new estimates. Ann Bot. 1997, 80: 169-196. 10.1006/anbo.1997.0415." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1472">14</a>]. We used a whole-genome shotgun (WGS) approach to generate ~43× coverage of sequence data from the Roche/454, Illumina, and SOLiD platforms (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1). Newbler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Margulies M, Egholm M, Altman WE, Attiya S, Bader JS, Bemben LA, Berka J, Braverman MS, Chen YJ, Chen Z, Dewell SB, Du L, Fierro JM, Gomes XV, Godwin BC, He W, Helgesen S, Ho CH, Irzyk GP, Jando SC, Alenquer ML, Jarvie TP, Jirage KB, Kim JB, Knight JR, Lanza JR, Leamon JH, Lefkowitz SM, Lei M, Li J: Genome sequencing in microfabricated high-density picolitre reactors. Nature. 2005, 437: 376-380." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1478">15</a>] was chosen as the assembler for the final assembly since the majority of the sequencing data came from the Roche/454 platform with relatively longer read length, especially for single end reads [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Barthelson R, McFarlin AJ, Rounsley SD, Young S: Plantagora: modeling whole genome sequencing and assembly of plant genomes. PLoS One. 2011, 6: e28436-10.1371/journal.pone.0028436." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1482">16</a>]. Repeat motif identification on preliminary assemblies, removal of repeat-matching raw sequencing reads, and stringent assembling parameters were applied. The final genome assembly, based on only 27.86 Gb data or ~13× coverage after filtering repeat-matching reads (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1), resulted in scaffolds totaling 1,119 Mb with an N50 of 2,972 bp (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#Tab1">1</a>). We anchored 143 scaffolds and the associated 1,325 genes onto the 18 <i>H. brasiliensis</i> linkage groups based on 154 microsatellite markers [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Le Guen V, Garcia D, Doaré F, Mattos CRR, Condina V, Couturier C, Chambon A, Weber C, Espéout S, Seguin M: A rubber tree’s durable resistance to Microcyclus ulei is conferred by a qualitative gene and a major quantitative resistance factor. Tree Genet Genomes. 2011, 7: 877-889. 10.1007/s11295-011-0381-7." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1494">17</a>] (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S1). Within the mapped scaffolds, 74 additional reported markers were also identified (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2). Most of the markers were located in the intergenic regions.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 
                          <b>Assembly and annotation statistics for the</b>
                          <b><i>H. brasiliensis</i></b>
                          <b>genome</b>
                        </b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We have exclusively used next-generation sequencing technologies for WGS assembly of the rubber tree genome, and only a few other plant genomes have taken a similar approach. The strawberry genome was sequenced using a similar combination of Roche/454, Illumina, and SOLiD reads (39× coverage) as in this study, but with a nearly 9× smaller genome (240 Mb) and considerably lower proportion of repetitive DNA (22%), much larger contigs/scaffolds could be achieved in that case [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Shulaev V, Sargent DJ, Crowhurst RN, Mockler TC, Folkerts O, Delcher AL, Jaiswal P, Mockaitis K, Liston A, Mane SP, Burns P, Davis TM, Slovin JP, Bassil N, Hellens RP, Evans C, Harkins T, Kodira C, Desany B, Crasta OR, Jensen RV, Allan AC, Michael TP, Setubal JC, Celton JM, Rees DJ, Williams KP, Holt SH, Ruiz Rojas JJ, Chatterjee M: The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat Genet. 2011, 43: 109-116. 10.1038/ng.740." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1776">18</a>]. On the other hand, the largest scaffolds we assembled (largest = 531.5 kb) were comparable with those of the cannabis genome (largest = 565.9 kb), which was assembled from Illumina and Roche/454 data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="van Bakel H, Stout JM, Cote AG, Tallon CM, Sharpe AG, Hughes TR, Page JE: The draft genome and transcriptome of Cannabis sativa. Genome Biol. 2011, 12: R102-10.1186/gb-2011-12-10-r102." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1779">19</a>]. The major challenge of assembling the rubber tree genome was due to its highly repetitive content. This was also a difficulty for the barley genome (5.1 Gb, 84% repetitive DNA), as the WGS assembly based on Illumina short reads resulted in relatively small contigs (N50 = 1,425 bp) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="The International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium: A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature. 2012, 491: 711-716." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1782">20</a>]. However, when combined with a BAC-based physical map and high-resolution genetic map, a highly structured chromosome-level framework was produced. Efforts such as incorporating a physical map or other methods to provide long-range linking information will be the next step for improving the rubber tree draft genome assembly.</p><p>Using RepeatModeler and RepeatMasker, 72.01% of the assembly was identified as repetitive DNA (excluding low complexity regions and RNA genes). This is estimated to represent ~78% of the genome, similar to that of maize (85%) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Schnable PS, Ware D, Fulton RS, Stein JC, Wei F, Pasternak S, Liang C, Zhang J, Fulton L, Graves TA, Minx P, Reily AD, Courtney L, Kruchowski SS, Tomlinson C, Strong C, Delehaunty K, Fronick C, Courtney B, Rock SM, Belter E, Du F, Kim K, Abbott RM, Cotton M, Levy A, Marchetto P, Ochoa K, Jackson SM, Gillam B: The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science. 2009, 326: 1112-1115. 10.1126/science.1178534." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1788">21</a>] and barley (84%) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="The International Barley Genome Sequencing Consortium: A physical, genetic and functional sequence assembly of the barley genome. Nature. 2012, 491: 711-716." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1791">20</a>]. Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons are the dominant class of transposable elements (46.15% of total repeats), of which the Gypsy-type (38.20%) and Copia-type (7.38%) are the most abundant (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3). Less than 2% of the total repeat elements are DNA transposons. A major part of the repeat elements (50.24%) could not be associated with any known families.</p><p>Combining the evidences derived from several <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction programs along with transcriptome and protein alignments, EVidenceModeler (EVM) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, White O, Buell CR, Wortman JR: Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 2008, 9: R7-10.1186/gb-2008-9-1-r7." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1803">22</a>] predicted 68,955 gene models from the masked assembly (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4). The average gene, exon, and intron lengths are 1,332 bp, 238 bp, and 332 bp, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#Tab1">1</a>). Of the 137,540 expressed sequence tags (ESTs) and assembled transcripts available for <i>H. brasiliensis</i>, 95.4% are represented in the genome (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S5). To provide additional support for gene model prediction and validation, we generated leaf transcriptome sequences (1,085 Mb using Roche/454 and 4.89 Gb using Illumina), which were <i>de novo</i> assembled into 73,060 contigs (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S6). Over 99% of these contigs and 81% of the Roche/454 transcriptome reads aligned to the genome assembly. These results indicate that the draft assembly represents a large proportion of the gene space.</p><p>Protein sequences from the final gene predictions were annotated through different databases, including the NCBI non-redundant database, SwissProt [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Boutet E, Lieberherr D, Tognolli M, Schneider M, Bairoch A: UniProtKB/Swiss-Prot. Methods Mol Biol. 2007, 406: 89-112." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1829">23</a>], InterPro [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zdobnov EM, Apweiler R: InterProScan - an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics. 2001, 17: 847-848. 10.1093/bioinformatics/17.9.847." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1832">24</a>], and KEGG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S, Kawashima S, Okuno Y, Hattori M: The KEGG resource for deciphering the genome. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004, 32: D277-D280. 10.1093/nar/gkh063." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1835">25</a>] (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S7). Eukaryotic orthologous groups (KOG) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Tatusov RL, Fedorova ND, Jackson JD, Jacobs AR, Kiryutin B, Koonin EV, Krylov DM, Mazumder R, Mekhedov SL, Nikolskaya AN, Rao BS, Smirnov S, Sverdlov AV, Vasudevan S, Wolf YI, Yin JJ, Natale DA: The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes. BMC Bioinformatics. 2003, 4: 41-10.1186/1471-2105-4-41." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1841">26</a>] analysis revealed a significantly higher number of proteins in the ‘signal transduction mechanisms’ (5,216), ‘posttranslational modifications, protein turnover, chaperones’ (2,886), and ‘carbohydrate transport and metabolism’ (1,665) categories (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S8). In addition, leucine-rich repeats (LRR) are the most abundant Pfam [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Finn RD, Mistry J, Schuster-Böckler B, Griffiths-Jones S, Hollich V, Lassmann T, Moxon S, Marshall M, Khanna A, Durbin R, Eddy SR, Sonnhammer EL, Bateman A: Pfam: clans, web tools and services. Nucleic Acids Res. 2006, 34: D247-D251. 10.1093/nar/gkj149." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1848">27</a>] domain represented in the genome (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S9). Among the gene models, 6.7% are predicted to have signal peptides, with the majority being plastidial and extracellular targeted (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10).</p><p>Other than protein-coding genes, we identified 729 tRNA genes including 12 suppressor (Sup) tRNAs, 32 pseudogenes, and 4 with undetermined function (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11). Clustering of tRNA genes was noticed and interestingly, all Sup tRNA genes were clustered into 2 scaffolds (9 in scaffold 134351 and 3 in scaffold 134362). We also identified 5S (113 copies), 5.8S (18 copies), 18S (11 copies), and 28S (21 copies) rRNA genes in the assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Phylogeny and lineage-specific genes</h3><p>Phylogenetic analysis using 144 single copy orthologous clusters from 17 sequenced plant genomes shows that <i>H. brasiliensis</i> shares the closest ancestry with <i>Manihot esculenta</i> (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#Fig1">1</a>), consistent with the placement based on chloroplast genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Tangphatsornruang S, Uthaipaisanwong P, Sangsrakru D, Chanprasert J, Yoocha T, Jomchai N, Tragoonrung S: Characterization of the complete chloroplast genome of Hevea brasiliensis reveals genome rearrangement, RNA editing sites and phylogenetic relationships. Gene. 2011, 475: 104-112. 10.1016/j.gene.2011.01.002." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1880">28</a>]. Outside the Euphorbiaceae, the closest sequenced genome is of <i>Populus. trichocarpa</i>. In agreement with the angiosperm phylogeny derived from 154 nuclear genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Shulaev V, Sargent DJ, Crowhurst RN, Mockler TC, Folkerts O, Delcher AL, Jaiswal P, Mockaitis K, Liston A, Mane SP, Burns P, Davis TM, Slovin JP, Bassil N, Hellens RP, Evans C, Harkins T, Kodira C, Desany B, Crasta OR, Jensen RV, Allan AC, Michael TP, Setubal JC, Celton JM, Rees DJ, Williams KP, Holt SH, Ruiz Rojas JJ, Chatterjee M: The genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca). Nat Genet. 2011, 43: 109-116. 10.1038/ng.740." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1887">18</a>], our analysis reveals that Malpighiales (includes Salicoid member <i>Populus</i> and the Euphorbiaceae members) shares a common ancestry with other members in Malvidae.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-75/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4747_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-75/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4747_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>Maximum likelihood phylogeny unveiling the taxonomic position of</b> <b><i>H. brasiliensis</i></b> <b>.</b> A phylogenetic tree was constructed using 144 orthologous single-copy gene clusters distributed across 17 species for which the genome sequences are available. The tree was constructed by the Maximum likelihood method using PhyML employing SPR and NNI for best tree improvement features. The analysis revealed the position of <i>H. brasiliensis</i> to be in Malvidae with more relatedness to <i>M. esculenta</i>. Bootstrapping procedures were applied over random 100 replicates and 7 seeds and the values are shown at nodes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We compared 13 representative plant genomes (grouped into Euphorbiaceae, dicots, monocots, and lower plants) and found that a core gene set of 7,140 clusters are common to all groups, while 9,516 are unique to the Euphorbiaceae (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#Fig2">2</a>a). Comparison of the four sequenced Euphorbiaceae genomes (<i>Jatropha, Ricinus, Manihot</i>, and <i>Hevea</i>) indicated that 2,708 clusters comprising 8,748 genes are <i>Hevea</i> specific (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#Fig2">2</a>b). We were able to assign 526 Gene Ontology (GO) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ashburner M, Ball CA, Blake JA, Botstein D, Butler H, Cherry JM, Davis AP, Dolinski K, Dwight SS, Eppig JT, Harris MA, Hill DP, Issel-Tarver L, Kasarskis A, Lewis S, Matese JC, Richardson JE, Ringwald M, Rubin GM, Sherlock G: Gene Ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat Genet. 2000, 25: 25-29. 10.1038/75556." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1944">29</a>] categories (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12), 266 InterPro domains (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13), and 267 Pfam domains (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S14) to these <i>Hevea</i> specific genes. The most abundant InterPro and Pfam domains belong to LRR and protein kinases. KOG analyses revealed that majority of the genes are associated with signal transduction, cytoskeleton, and posttranslational modification (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S15).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-75/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4747_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-75/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4747_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>Venn diagrams showing the distribution of unique and shared gene families.</b> OrthoMCL was used to identify gene clusters across 13 plant species (<b>a</b>) as well as between the four sequenced Euphorbiaceae members (<b>b</b>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Rubber biosynthesis</h3><p>Rubber biosynthesis involves fixation of carbon in the leaf, loading and transportation of the assimilates, specialized metabolic processes driving the precursors for biosynthesis, and storage of polyisoprenes in the laticifer. Sucrose provides the carbon skeleton and energy supply for rubber biosynthesis, with laticifers serving as its strong sink [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Silpi U, Lacointe A, Kasempsap P, Thanysawanyangkura S, Chantuma P, Gohet E, Musigamart N, Clément A, Améglio T, Thaler P: Carbohydrate reserves as a competing sink: evidence from tapping rubber trees. Tree Physiol. 2007, 27: 881-889. 10.1093/treephys/27.6.881." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d7890e1997">30</a>]. We reconstructed the entire metabolic pathway of rubber biosynthesis in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2). The carbon assimilatory mechanism of rubber biosynthesis consists of 12 distinct sub-metabolic pathways (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#Fig3">3</a>), represented by 417 genes (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S16). We validated the expression of these genes from leaf and/or latex cDNA pools, detecting at least one isoform for the majority of the gene families (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S16). We also made a comparison with the ESTs obtained from the rubber-producing bark tissue of guayule [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Ponciano G, McMahan CM, Xie W, Lazo GR, Coffelt TA, Collins-Silva J, Nural-Taban A, Gollery M, Shintani DK, Whalen MC: Transcriptome and gene expression analysis in cold-acclimated guayule (Parthenium argentatum) rubber-producing tissue. Phytochemistry. 2012, 79: 57-66." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2016">31</a>]. We found that 360 of the <i>H. brasiliensis</i> genes were represented in the guayule ESTs, of which 205 showed more than 70% sequence identity with the best match (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S17).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-75/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4747_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-75/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4747_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>Schematic representation of the metabolic pathway leading to natural rubber biosynthesis.</b> Import of sucrose until biosynthesis of rubber involves 12 sub-metabolic pathways represented in the large boxes. The number of enzymes and associated proteins in each individual pathway is shown in small white boxes and the number of orthologs in <i>Hevea</i> in the grey boxes. The detailed pathway is shown in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It has been shown that sucrose transporters and their expression patterns are directly related to tapping and rubber production [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Tang C, Huang D, Yang J, Liu S, Sakr S, Li H, Zhou Y, Qin Y: The sucrose transporter HbSUT3 plays an active role in sucrose loading to laticifer and rubber productivity in exploited trees of Hevea brasiliensis (para rubber tree). Plant Cell Environ. 2010, 33: 1708-1720. 10.1111/j.1365-3040.2010.02175.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2054">32</a>]. Sucrose and monosaccharides are imported into the laticifer cytosol via sucrose (SUT) and monosaccharide (MT) transporters which are encoded by 7 and 30 genes, respectively. β-fructofuranosidase and fructan β-fructosidase convert sucrose into monosaccharides, and the high number of genes (31) found in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> indicates the importance of this function for rubber biosynthesis. Excess sucrose is stored as fructan and starch which can later be used as a carbon source for rubber biosynthesis. Fructan metabolism consists of 9 enzymatic steps (encoded by 54 genes), while starch metabolism involves 11 reactions (encoded by 38 genes). Carbon is directed through glycolysis (encoded by 52 genes), alternative pentose phosphate pathway (encoded by 14 genes), and acetyl CoA biosynthetic pathway (encoded by 120 genes) to produce intermediate substrates for the biosynthesis of rubber precursors.</p><p>Isoprenoid precursors for rubber biosynthesis are provided by the cytosolic mevalonate dependent (MVA) pathway in the form of IPP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Kekwick RGO: The formation of isoprenoids in Hevea latex. Physiology of Rubber Tree Latex. Edited by: d’Auzac J, Jacob JL, Chrestin L. 1989, Boca Raton: CRC Press, 145-164." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2063">33</a>]. The plastidic mevalonate independent (MEP) pathway is also suggested to contribute IPP for rubber biosynthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Ko JH, Chow KS, Han KH: Transcriptome analysis reveals novel features of the molecular events occurring in the laticifers of Hevea brasiliensis (para rubber tree). Plant Mol Biol. 2003, 53: 479-492." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2066">34</a>]. Recently, <sup>13</sup>C-labelled studies on <i>Hevea</i> seedlings suggested that the MEP pathway contributes IPP for carotenoid biosynthesis, but not for rubber biosynthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Sando T, Takeno S, Watanabe N, Okumoto H, Kuzuyama T, Yamashita A, Hattori M, Ogasawara N, Fukusaki E, Kobayashi A: Cloning and characterization of the 2-C-methyl-D-erythritol 4-phosphate (MEP) pathway genes of a natural-rubber producing plant. Hevea brasiliensis. Biosci Biotechnol Biochem. 2008, 72: 2903-2917. 10.1271/bbb.80387." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2074">35</a>]. However, expression analysis on MVA and MEP pathway genes suggest that the MEP pathway can be an alternate provider of IPP in mature rubber trees or in clones which do not produce a large amount of carotenoid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chow KS, Mat-Isa MN, Bahari A, Ghazali AK, Alias H, Mohd-Zainuddin Z, Hoh CC, Wan KL: Metabolic routes affecting rubber biosynthesis in Hevea brasiliensis latex. J Exp Bot. 2012, 63: 1863-1871. 10.1093/jxb/err363." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2078">8</a>]. In the <i>H. brasiliensis</i> genome, we identified 18 genes encoding enzymes for the MVA pathway and 29 for the MEP pathway. For the initiation of rubber biosynthesis, a priming allylic diphosphate (farnesyl diphosphate, geranylgeranyl diphosphate, or undecaprenyl diphosphate) is needed [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Rattanapittayaporn A, Wititsuwannakul D, Wititsuwannakul R: Significant role of bacterial undecaprenyl diphosphate (C55-UPP) for rubber synthesis by Hevea latex enzymes. Macromol Biosci. 2004, 4: 1039-1052. 10.1002/mabi.200400096." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2084">36</a>]. The biosynthetic pathway leading to these compounds involves 5 enzymatic steps encoded by 21 genes in the assembly.</p><p>Rubber polymerases, involved in the polymerization of isoprenoids, belong to the family of CPTs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kharel Y, Koyama T: Molecular analysis of cis-prenyl chain elongating enzymes. Nat Prod Rep. 2003, 20: 111-118. 10.1039/b108934j." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2090">37</a>]. We identified eight CPTs from the genome (designated as CPT 1–8) which are divided into three groups according to evolutionary relationships (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#Fig4">4</a>). We found that <i>H. brasiliensis</i> CPTs in groups 2 and 3 are homologous to other plant CPTs (undecaprenyl pyrophosphate synthase and dehydrodolichyl diphosphate synthase) which are responsible for the elongation of short-chain C5-isoprenes (C<sub>55</sub> – C<sub>120</sub>). Group 1, comprising CPT 1–3, is specific to <i>H. brasiliensis</i> and members belonging to this group were proven to catalyze the formation of long chain C5-isoprenes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Asawatreratanakul K, Zhang YW, Wititsuwannakul D, Wititsuwannakul R, Takahashi S, Rattanapittayaporn A, Koyama T: Molecular cloning, expression and characterization of cDNA encoding cis-prenyltransferases from Hevea brasiliensis: a key factor participating in natural rubber biosynthesis. Eur J Biochem. 2003, 270: 4671-4680. 10.1046/j.1432-1033.2003.03863.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2107">4</a>]. Only CPT 4 (group 3) has introns and it shares the least homology with others. Small rubber particle protein (SRPP) and rubber elongation factor (REF) are two other key proteins involved in rubber biosynthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Gronover CS, Wahler D, Prüfer D: Natural rubber biosynthesis and physic-chemical studies on plant derived latex. Biotechnology of Biopolymers. Edited by: Elnashar M. 2011, Croatia: Intech Open Acess Publisher, 75-88." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2110">5</a>] and are represented by 10 and 12 genes, respectively, in our assembly.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-75/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4747_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-75/MediaObjects/12864_2012_Article_4747_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>Phylogenetic analysis of plant CPTs.</b> The evolutionary history was inferred by the Maximum likelihood method using MEGA5.05 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Tamura K, Peterson D, Peterson N, Stecher G, Nei M, Kumar S: MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol Biol Evol. 2011, 28: 2731-2739. 10.1093/molbev/msr121." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2125">38</a>]. All positions containing gaps and missing data were eliminated. CPT, <i>cis</i>-prenyltransferase; UPPS, undecaprenyl pyrophosphate synthase; and DHDDS, dehydrodolichyl diphosphate synthase. Bootstrapping values (100 replicates) are shown on branches.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Rubberwood</h3><p>Matured rubber trees that have reached the end of their latex-producing cycle are used as a source of timber for the manufacture of furniture and other products. Wood quality is associated with several lignocellulose biosynthesis genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Dillon SK, Nolan M, Li W, Bell C, Wu HX, Southerton SG: Allelic variation in cell wall candidate genes affecting solid wood properties in natural populations and land races of Pinus radiata. Genetics. 2010, 185: 1477-1487. 10.1534/genetics.110.116582." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2147">39</a>], and we identified 127 genes in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S18). There are 36 cellulose synthase (CesA)-coding genes compared with 10 in <i>Arabidopsis</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Richmond TA, Somerville CR: The cellulose synthase superfamily. Plant Physiol. 2000, 124: 495-498. 10.1104/pp.124.2.495." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2159">40</a>] and 18 in <i>Populus</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Djerbi S, Lindskog M, Arvestad L, Sterky F, Teeri TT: The genome sequence of black cottonwood (Populus trichocarpa) reveals 18 conserved cellulose synthase (CesA) genes. Planta. 2005, 221: 739-746. 10.1007/s00425-005-1498-4." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2166">41</a>]. Genes associated with hemicellulose biosynthesis have been identified in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> and is similar to <i>Populus</i> in having more α-L-fucosidases over α-L-fucosyltransferases [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Tuskan GA, Difazio S, Jansson S, Bohlmann J, Grigoriev I, Hellsten U, Putnam N, Ralph S, Rombauts S, Salamov A, Schein J, Sterck L, Aerts A, Bhalerao RR, Bhalerao RP, Blaudez D, Boerjan W, Brun A, Brunner A, Busov V, Campbell M, Carlson J, Chalot M, Chapman J, Chen GL, Cooper D, Coutinho PM, Couturier J, Covert S, Cronk Q: The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science. 2006, 313: 1596-1604. 10.1126/science.1128691." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2175">42</a>]. Lignin, a heteropolymer of monolignols, determines the texture and hardness of the wood. The complete set of genes involved in monolignol biosynthesis (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S3) showed the highest similarity with <i>Populus</i> genes. In comparison with the <i>Populus</i> genome, caffeic acid O-methyl transferase (COMT) and cinnamyl alcohol dehydrogenase (CAD) showed noticeable difference in their numbers (COMT: 10 in <i>Hevea</i>, 41 in <i>Populus</i>; CAD: 5 in <i>Hevea</i>, 24 in <i>Populus</i>). This is probably related to the hardness of <i>Populus</i> wood [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Dillon SK, Nolan M, Li W, Bell C, Wu HX, Southerton SG: Allelic variation in cell wall candidate genes affecting solid wood properties in natural populations and land races of Pinus radiata. Genetics. 2010, 185: 1477-1487. 10.1534/genetics.110.116582." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2204">39</a>] compared with rubberwood. The genes involved in transport, storage, and mobilization of monolignols and its final polymerization into lignin have also been identified.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Disease resistance</h3><p>Rubber trees are highly susceptible to fungal diseases, so the identification of disease resistance genes is one of the major focuses of rubber tree research. Hypersensitive response (HR) is the early defense response that causes necrosis and cell death to restrict the growth of the pathogen. Plant signaling molecules, salicylic and jasmonic acids, play a critical role in activating systemic acquired resistance (SAR) and induce certain pathogenesis-related (PR) proteins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Durrant WE, Dong X: Systemic acquired resistance. Annu Rev Phytopathol. 2004, 42: 185-209. 10.1146/annurev.phyto.42.040803.140421." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2216">43</a>]. The nucleotide-binding site (NBS)-coding R gene family is the largest group of disease resistance genes in plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Mun JH, Yu HJ, Park S, Park BS: Genome-wide identification of NBS-encoding resistance genes in Brassica rapa. Mol Genet Genomics. 2009, 282: 617-631. 10.1007/s00438-009-0492-0." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2219">44</a>]. In <i>H. brasiliensis</i> we identified 618 members in this family, comparable to <i>Oryza sativa</i>, which are divided into 6 sub-classes: toll-interleukin-like receptor (TIR)-NBS, coiled-coil (CC)-NBS, NBS, TIR-NBS-LRR, CC-NBS-LRR, and NBS-LRR (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S19). The majority were those without LRR domains, in contrast with other plants where the LRR-containing classes are typically more abundant. We also identified 147 PR and 96 early defense (SAR and HR) associated genes in the assembly (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Tables S20 and S21). All these disease resistance genes were distributed in 665 scaffolds, and NBS-coding genes were often found to be in clusters (<i>e.g</i>., 9 NBS-LRR genes in scaffold 409956). In addition, we have reconstructed the SAR and HR signaling pathways for <i>H. brasiliensis</i> (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM2">2</a>: Figures S4 and S5). The overall information can be potentially exploited for the biotic stress management of the plant.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Latex allergens</h3><p>Allergy to natural rubber latex (NRL) is one of the major global medical concerns. There are 14 internationally recognized NRL allergens, known as Hevb 1 to Hevb 14 (<a href="http://www.allergen.org">http://www.allergen.org</a>). These are encoded by 100 genes in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S22). Most of the allergens are stress and defense-related proteins highly abundant in the latex [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Yeang HY, Arif SAM, Yusof F, Sunderasan E: Allergenic protein of natural rubber latex. Methods. 2002, 27: 32-45. 10.1016/S1046-2023(02)00049-X." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2265">45</a>]. Of the major allergens causing sensitization to NRL, Hevb 6 (hevein) is encoded by 16 genes whereas Hevb 5 is only a single copy gene. Hevb 1 (REF) and Hevb 3 (SRPP), associated with rubber particle, are represented by 12 and 10 genes, respectively. Hevb 4 (lecithinase homolog) with 5 genes and Hevb 13 (esterase) with 9 genes are also known as glycoallergens. There are 6, 4, and 2 genes coding for the cross-reactivity proteins Hevb 8 (profilin), Hevb 9 (enolase), and Hevb 10 (manganese superoxide dismutase), respectively. Defense-related allergens Hevb 2 (β-1,3-glucanase) and Hevb 11 (chitinase) are with 11 genes each. Domain analysis of Hevb 11 shows the presence of an 18–23 amino acid long signal peptide which confers the systemic wounding response in plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Ryan CA, Moura DS: Systemic wound signaling in plants: a new perception. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA. 2002, 99: 6519-6520. 10.1073/pnas.112196499." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2268">46</a>]. Other than the aforementioned latex allergens, 4 types of non-latex allergens (pollen allergen, α-expansin, β-expansin, and isoflavone reductase) were also identified in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S23).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Transcription factors</h3><p>The <i>H. brasiliensis</i> genome contains ~6000 transcription factors distributed in 50 major families (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S24). Transcription factors account for 8.5% of gene models in <i>H. brasiliensis</i>. The bHLH, MYB, C3H, G2-like, and WRKY families are overrepresented. bHLH, the largest transcription factor family in most plants, is represented by 752 members. MYB, a diverse family of transcription factors that co-interacts with the bHLH family to regulate secondary metabolism [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Feller A, Machemer K, Braun EL, Grotewold E: Evolutionary and comparative analysis of MYB and bHLH plant transcription factors. Plant J. 2011, 6: 94-116." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2295">47</a>] as well as biotic and abiotic stress, has 570 members. The C3H family, involved in floral development, embryogenesis, wintering and leaf senescence [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Wang D, Guo Y, Wu C, Yang G, Li Y, Zheng C: Genome-wide analysis of CCCH zinc finger family in Arabidopsis and rice. BMC Genomics. 2008, 9: 44-10.1186/1471-2164-9-44." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2298">48</a>], is represented by 470 members followed by G2-like (461; photosynthetic regulation) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Riaño-Pachón DM, Corrêa LGG, Trejos-Espinosa R, Mueller-Roeber B: Green transcription factors: a Chlamydomonas overview. Genetics. 2008, 179: 31-39. 10.1534/genetics.107.086090." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2302">49</a>] and WRKY (445; immune responses) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Rushton PJ, Somssich IE, Ringler P, Shen QJ: WRKY transcription factors. Trends Plant Sci. 2010, 15: 247-258. 10.1016/j.tplants.2010.02.006." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2305">50</a>]. MADS-box genes encoding homeotic floral transcription factors are divided into 5 groups, Mα, Mβ, Mγ, Mδ (or MIKC*), and MIKC<sup>c</sup>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Arora R, Agarwal P, Ray S, Singh AK, Singh VP, Tyagi AK, Kapoor S: MADS-box gene family in rice: genome-wide identification, organization and expression profiling during reproductive development and stress. BMC Genomics. 2007, 8: 242-10.1186/1471-2164-8-242." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2310">51</a>], and are represented by 112 members. There are 79 Type II MADS-box (Mδ and MIKC<sup>c</sup> groups) genes in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> while the number is 54–67 in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>Populus</i>, and <i>Oryza</i>. In contrast, only 33 Type I MADS-box (Mα, Mβ, and Mγ groups) genes are in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> compared to 29–94 present in the other 3 species. Only 12.5% (14 out of 112) MADS-box genes in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> were clustered compared to 47% in the <i>C. papaya</i> genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Ming R, Hou S, Feng Y, Yu Q, Dionne-Laporte A, Saw JH, Senin P, Wang W, Ly BV, Lewis KL, Salzberg SL, Feng L, Jones MR, Skelton RL, Murray JE, Chen C, Qian W, Shen J, Du P, Eustice M, Tong E, Tang H, Lyons E, Paull RE, Michael TP, Wall K, Rice DW, Albert H, Wang ML, Zhu YJ: The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature. 2008, 452: 991-996. 10.1038/nature06856." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2338">52</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Phytohormone biosynthesis and signaling</h3><p>Phytohormone biosynthetic and signaling-related genes are well represented in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S25; in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM2">2</a>: Figures S6, S7, S8, S9, S10, S11, S12, S13, S14, S15, S16, S17). Angiosperms dedicate a larger proportion of their genomes to auxin signaling, as evident by 12 gene families. However, in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> a significant reduction in the number is observed for some of the auxin gene family members compared to other plants, especially for SAUR and IAA repressors. GA-20-oxidase, a key regulatory enzyme in gibberellin biosynthesis, has 5 orthologs in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> compared to one in <i>Ricinu</i> s and <i>Oryza.</i> The ethylene-responsive element binding factor (ERF) proteins are overrepresented (246 orthologs) in <i>H. brasiliensis</i>, compared to other plants. The increased number of ERF transcription factors may be involved in the ethylene-dependent processes specific to <i>H. brasiliensis</i>. Oxophytodienoic acid reductase, important in the jasmonic acid biosynthesis pathway, is encoded by 13 genes. Nitric oxide synthase, involved in the biosynthesis of nitric oxide as a defense mechanism, is a highly conserved single copy gene in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>Ricinus</i>, <i>Populus</i>, and <i>Oryza</i>, whereas in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> there are 4 copies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Light signaling and circadian clock related genes</h3><p>Light signaling pathways and circadian clocks are interconnected and have profound effects on the plant’s physiology. Light is one of the most important environmental signals processed by the circadian clock to synchronize appropriate timing of physiological events [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Más P: Circadian clock signaling in Arabidopsis thaliana: from gene expression to physiology and development. Int J Dev Biol. 2005, 49: 491-500. 10.1387/ijdb.041968pm." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2401">53</a>]. Expansion in the number of genes involved in photoperception and circadian rhythm is observed in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> (154), compared to <i>Populus</i> (77) and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (66) (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S26). These results indicate the intense involvement of environmental signals in regulating the physiology of the rubber tree.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">F-box proteins</h3><p>F-box proteins are part of the Skp1p-cullin-F-box protein complex involved in the ubiquitin/26S proteasome pathway responsible for the selective degradation of proteins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Jain M, Nijhawan A, Arora R, Agarwal P, Ray S, Sharma P, Kapoor S, Tyagi AK, Khurana JP: F-box proteins in rice. Genome-wide analysis, classification, temporal and spatial gene expression during panicle and seed development, and regulation by light and abiotic stress. Plant Physiol. 2007, 143: 1467-1483. 10.1104/pp.106.091900." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2424">54</a>]. They are characterized by a conserved F-box domain (40–50 amino acids) at the N-terminus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Jain M, Nijhawan A, Arora R, Agarwal P, Ray S, Sharma P, Kapoor S, Tyagi AK, Khurana JP: F-box proteins in rice. Genome-wide analysis, classification, temporal and spatial gene expression during panicle and seed development, and regulation by light and abiotic stress. Plant Physiol. 2007, 143: 1467-1483. 10.1104/pp.106.091900." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2427">54</a>] and are reported to be involved in the regulation of various developmental processes in plants such as leaf senescence, flowering, branching, phytochrome and phytohormone signaling, circadian rhythms, and self-incompatibility [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Yang X, Kalluri UC, Jawdy S, Gunter LE, Yin T, Tschaplinski TJ, Weston DJ, Ranjan P, Tuskan GA: The F-box gene family is expanded in herbaceous annual plants relative to woody perennial plants. Plant Physiol. 2008, 148: 1189-1200. 10.1104/pp.108.121921." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2430">55</a>]. In <i>H. brasiliensis</i> there are 655 F-box genes, compared to 315 in <i>V. vinifera</i>, 198 in <i>C. papaya</i>, 425 in <i>P. trichocarpa,</i> 897 in <i>A. thaliana</i>, and 971 in <i>O. sativa</i>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Hua Z, Zou C, Shiu SH, Vierstra RD: Phylogenetic comparison of F-box (FBX) gene superfamily within the plant kingdom reveals divergent evolutionary histories indicative of genomic drift. PLoS One. 2011, 6: e16219-10.1371/journal.pone.0016219." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2452">56</a>]. This is interesting and contrary to the belief that the F-box gene family is expanded in herbaceous annuals compared to woody perennials [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Yang X, Kalluri UC, Jawdy S, Gunter LE, Yin T, Tschaplinski TJ, Weston DJ, Ranjan P, Tuskan GA: The F-box gene family is expanded in herbaceous annual plants relative to woody perennial plants. Plant Physiol. 2008, 148: 1189-1200. 10.1104/pp.108.121921." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2455">55</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Carotenoids</h3><p>Carotenoids have a pivotal role in light harvesting, photoprotection, photomorphogenesis, lipid peroxidation, and a vast array of plant developmental processes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Chaudhary N, Nijhawan A, Khurana JP, Khurana P: Carotenoid biosynthesis genes in rice: structural analysis, genome-wide expression profiling and phylogenetic analysis. Mol Genet Genomics. 2010, 283: 13-33. 10.1007/s00438-009-0495-x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2467">57</a>]. Carotenoids are found in nearly all types of plastids including the Frey-Wyssling particles of rubber latex, imparting yellowish color to the latex of some clones. Although the role of carotenoids in the latex is not well-defined, it could be a competing sink for IPP in the laticifers. IPP from the MEP pathway is proposed to be utilized for <i>cis</i>-polyisoprene synthesis in clones having low carotenoid content in the latex [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chow KS, Mat-Isa MN, Bahari A, Ghazali AK, Alias H, Mohd-Zainuddin Z, Hoh CC, Wan KL: Metabolic routes affecting rubber biosynthesis in Hevea brasiliensis latex. J Exp Bot. 2012, 63: 1863-1871. 10.1093/jxb/err363." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2473">8</a>]. It is observed that the genes for the carotenoid biosynthetic pathway in <i>H. brasiliensis</i> (48) underwent an expansion compared to the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome (28) (in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S27; in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S18). Phytoene synthase and phytoene desaturase, the enzymes catalyzing the initial committed steps of carotenoid biosynthesis, are highly expanded in <i>Hevea</i> with 5 and 9 genes, compared to single copies in <i>Arabidopsis.</i> The overall observations indicate more efficient carotenoid biosynthetic machinery in <i>Hevea</i>, possibly with diverse functions.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><p>Given the pivotal roles of NR production and sustainability, this draft genome sequence is an invaluable resource added to the spurge family. It will facilitate and accelerate the genetic improvement of <i>H. brasiliensis</i> through molecular breeding and exploitation of genetic resources. We observed the occurrence of a higher percentage (~78%) of repeat elements which could be attributed to the increased rate of non-homologous recombination and exon shuffling [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Yang S, Arguello JR, Li X, Ding Y, Zhou Q, Chen Y, Zhang Y, Zhao R, Brunet F, Peng L, Long M, Wang W: Repetitive element-mediated recombination as a mechanism for new gene origination in Drosophila. PLoS Genet. 2008, 4: e3-10.1371/journal.pgen.0040003." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-75#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d7890e2510">58</a>], thereby reducing the consistency in the genetic purity of the progeny. The high percentage of repeat elements together with a lack of chromosome level information is the major hurdle in assembling the whole <i>H. brasiliensis</i> genome. The genome information together with the characterization of all available molecular markers linked to the desired genes will facilitate NR production by means of trait dependent molecular breeding. Alongside tree genome sequences available from <i>Populus</i>, <i>Eucalyptus</i>, and the herbaceous model <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rubber research would specifically get assistance in the key areas of latex production, wood development, disease resistance, and allergenicity.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>High quality chromosomal DNA was extracted from young leaves of <i>H. brasiliensis</i> RRIM 600. Shotgun and paired-end (PE) libraries were prepared following the manufacturer’s instructions. High quality reads were generated by Illumina (200 bp and 500 bp PE), Roche/454 (shotgun, 8 kb PE, 20 kb PE), and SOLiD (2 kb PE) sequencers.</p><p>Preliminary genome assemblies were generated by two assemblers designed for <i>de novo</i> assembly of next-generation sequencing data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Miller JR, Koren S, Sutton G: Assembly algorithms for next-generation sequencing data.
